Let's first find out at what price will the stamp be offered now, which is after 3 years.
The stamp's offer price doubles every 3 years. So, in 3 years the stamp's offer price will be $2\times\$1.25 = \$2.50$.
After that, in the next 3 years, the stamp's offer price will again double to become $2\times\$2.50 = \$5$.
Similarly, in the next 3 years, the offer price will double again to $\$10$, and in the next 3 years, it will double again to $\$20$.

Now, we need to find what the stamp's offer price will be in 12 more years (which is after 3+3+3+3=12 years from now). Since the offer price doubles every 3 years, the number of times it will double in the next 12 years is 12/3 = 4 times.

So, starting from $\$1.25$, the stamp's offer price will double 4 times to reach:

$\$1.25\times2\times2\times2\times2 = \$20$
Therefore, Mike will be offered $\$20$ in 12 more years.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, here's how you can solve the problem:

Expanding the left side of the equation, we have:

$$(2x+5)(x-3) = 2x^2 - 6x + 5x - 15$$
$$= 2x^2 - x - 15$$

So we can rewrite the equation as:

$$2x^2 - x - 15 = 14$$

Subtracting 14 from both sides:

$$2x^2 - x - 29 = 0$$

To solve for $x$, we can use the quadratic formula:

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

Where $a = 2$, $b = -1$, and $c = -29$. Plugging in these values, we get:

$$x = \frac{-(-1) \pm \sqrt{(-1)^2 - 4(2)(-29)}}{2(2)}$$

Simplifying:

$$x = \frac{1 \pm \sqrt{1 + 232}}{4}$$
$$= \frac{1 \pm \sqrt{233}}{4}$$

So the possible values of $x$ are:

$$x = \frac{1 + \sqrt{233}}{4} \quad \text{or} \quad x = \frac{1 - \sqrt{233}}{4}$$

And the sum of these values is:

$$\frac{1 + \sqrt{233}}{4} + \frac{1 - \sqrt{233}}{4} = \frac{2}{4} = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$$

I hope this helps!

Since the points $(-1,0)$ and $(5,0)$ have the same $y$-value, the axis of symmetry of the parabola must be between these 2 points.  The $x$-value halfway between $-1$ and $5$ is $x=2$.  Therefore the vertex of the parabola is equal to $(2,k)$ for some $k$ and the parabola may also be written as  \[a(x-2)^2+k.\] Now we substitute.  The point $(5,0)$ gives  \[0=a(5-2)^2+k,\] or  \[9a+k=0.\] The point $(0,5)$ gives  \[5=a(0-2)^2+k\] or  \[4a+k=5.\] Subtracting the second equation from the first gives  \[(9a+k)-(4a+k)=0-5\] so $5a=-5$, giving $a=-1$.

Since $a=-1$ and $9a+k=0$ we know that $k=9$ and our parabola is  \[ax^2+bx+c=-(x-2)^2+9.\] In order to compute $100a+10b+c$ we can substitute $x=10$ and that gives  \[100a+10b+c=-(10-2)^2+9=\boxed{-55}.\]

We can start by setting up a system of three equations to solve for the coefficients $a$, $b$, and $c$. We know that the parabola passes through the points $(-1,0)$, $(0,5)$, and $(5,0)$, so we can substitute these coordinates into the general equation of a parabola to get:

\begin{align*}
a(-1)^2 + b(-1) + c &= 0 \\
a(0)^2 + b(0) + c &= 5 \\
a(5)^2 + b(5) + c &= 0
\end{align*}

Simplifying each equation, we get:

\begin{align*}
a - b + c &= 0 \\
c &= 5 \\
25a + 5b + c &= 0
\end{align*}

Substituting $c=5$ into the first equation, we get $a-b+5=0$, or $a=b-5$. Substituting this into the third equation, we get $25(b-5)+5b+5=0$, or $30b=130$, or $b=\frac{13}{3}$.

Substituting $b=\frac{13}{3}$ and $c=5$ into the first equation, we get $a-\frac{13}{3}+5=0$, or $a=\frac{4}{3}$.

Therefore, $100a+10b+c = 100\left(\frac{4}{3}\right)+10\left(\frac{13}{3}\right)+5 = \boxed{165}$.

We notice that the first term is 13 and the common difference is 7. Let the $n$th term of this sequence be $a_n$. The formula for the $n$th term of an arithmetic sequence with first term $a_1$ and common difference $d$ is $a_n = a_1 + (n-1)d$. Thus, we have that $a_n = 13 + 7(n-1)$. 

Now we want to find values of $n$ such that $a_n \leq 2008$. Substituting in the formula, we have $13 + 7(n-1) \leq 2008 \Rightarrow 7(n-1) \leq 1995 \Rightarrow n-1 \leq 285$. Thus, $n \leq 286$. 

On the other hand, we want $a_n$ to be positive, so we have $13 + 7(n-1) \geq 0 \Rightarrow n \geq 2$. 

Putting these together, we have $2 \leq n \leq 286$, so there are $\boxed{285}$ integers in this sequence.

- a₃ = a₂ + 1010
- a₄ = a₃ + 1010
- ...

We can rewrite this pattern using a formula:

aₙ = 2222 + 1010(n-1)

Where n is the term number.

So to find the sixth term, we can substitute n=6:

a₆ = 2222 + 1010(6-1)
a₆ = 7272

And to find the seventh term, we substitute n=7:

a₇ = 2222 + 1010(7-1)
a₇ = 8282

Now we just need to find the sum of these two terms:

a₆ + a₇ = 7272 + 8282
a₆ + a₇ = 15554

Therefore, the sum of the sixth and seventh terms of the sequence is 15554.

I hope this helps! Good luck with your precalculus class.

Certainly! We can use the method of substitution to solve this problem. 

First, let's solve the second equation for $x$ in terms of $a$, so that we can substitute it into the first equation. Using the quadratic formula, we have:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4x^2+8x+3 &= 0 \\
x &= \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{8^2 - 4 \cdot 4 \cdot 3}}{2 \cdot 4} \\
x &= \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{4}}{8} \\
x &= \frac{-2 \pm 1}{4}
\end{aligned}
$$

So, $x = -\frac{3}{4}$ or $x = -\frac{1}{4}$. Let's substitute these values into the first equation and see which one satisfies it:

$$
\begin{aligned}
18x^2+25x-3 &= 0 \\
18(-\frac{3}{4})^2+25(-\frac{3}{4})-3 &= 0 \\
-\frac{27}{2} - \frac{75}{4} - 3 &= 0 \\
-27 - \frac{75}{2} - 12 &= 0
\end{aligned}
$$
This equation does not have an integral solution. 

Now, let's try $x = -\frac{1}{4}$: 

$$
\begin{aligned}
18x^2+25x-3 &= 0 \\
18(-\frac{1}{4})^2+25(-\frac{1}{4})-3 &= 0 \\
\frac{9}{8} - \frac{25}{4} - 3 &= 0 \\
\frac{9}{8} - \frac{50}{8} - \frac{24}{8} &= 0 \\
-\frac{65}{8} &= 0
\end{aligned}
$$

This equation does not have an integral solution. Therefore, there is no value of x that satisfies both equations.

By the definition of inverse proportion, the product $jk$ is always equal to some constant $C$.  Substituting the given values, we can see that $42\cdot 56= 2352=C$.  Using this $C$ value, we can solve for $j$ when $k=32$: \begin{align*}
j\cdot 32&=2352\\
\Rightarrow\qquad j&=\frac{2352}{32}=\boxed{73.5}
\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's solve this problem together.

We know that $j$ and $k$ are inversely proportional. This means that as $j$ increases, $k$ decreases and vice versa. We can write this relationship as:

$$j \cdot k = c$$

where $c$ is a constant. This means that if we know the value of $j$ and $k$ for one situation, we can solve for the constant $c$ and use it to solve for other situations.

In this case, we know that when $j=42$, $k=56$. We can use this information to solve for the constant $c$:

$$j \cdot k = c$$
$$42 \cdot 56 = c$$
$$c = 2352$$

Now we can use this constant to solve for $j$ when $k=32$:

$$j \cdot k = c$$
$$j \cdot 32 = 2352$$
$$j = \frac{2352}{32}$$
$$j \approx 73.5$$

First, let's graph the two equations to get a visual representation of the region:

![Graph of y=|x| and y=-x^2+35/4](https://i.imgur.com/F6Ua3ZU.png)

The region we're interested in is the one between the two curves. 

Now, let's focus on the $y=|x|$ curve first. This curve splits the region into two parts: one where $y\geq0$ and $x\geq0$, and another where $y\geq0$ and $x\leq0$. 

Let's focus on the first part, where $x\geq0$. We can see that the curve $y=|x|$ intersects the $y$-axis at $(0,0)$, and the $x$-axis at $(1,1)$ and $(-1,1)$. This means that there are 3 lattice points on the curve in this part of the region: $(0,0)$, $(1,1)$, and $(-1,1)$.

Now, let's move on to the $y=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ curve. This is a parabola that opens downwards, and we can see that it intersects the $y$-axis at $(0,\frac{35}{4})$. To find the $x$-intercepts of this curve, we can set $y=0$ and solve for $x$. Doing so gives us:

$$0=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$$
$$x=\pm\frac{\sqrt{35}}{2}$$

Since $\sqrt{35}$ is not an integer, we don't have any lattice points on the curve where $y=0$. However, we do have some lattice points inside the parabola. To find these points, we can test out different values of $x$ and see if the corresponding $y$ value is an integer. 

For example, if we let $x=2$, we get:

$$y=-2^2+\frac{35}{4}=-\frac{3}{4}$$

Since $-\frac{3}{4}$ is not an integer, this point is not a lattice point. However, if we let $x=1$, we get:

$$y=-1^2+\frac{35}{4}=\frac{31}{4}$$

Since $\frac{31}{4}$ is an integer, the point $(1, \frac{31}{4})$ is a lattice point inside the parabola. We can repeat this process for other integer values of $x$ to find other lattice points inside the parabola. 

Putting everything together, we have a total of 3 lattice points on the $y=|x|$ curve and some number of lattice points inside the parabola. This means that our final answer is at least 3, but we need to find out how many lattice points are inside the parabola.

To do this, we need to find the range of $x$ values that are inside the parabola. We know that the parabola intersects the $x$-axis at $\pm\frac{\sqrt{35}}{2}$, so any $x$ value outside of this range will not have a corresponding $y$ value inside the parabola. Therefore, we only need to test integer values of $x$ between $-3$ and $3$ (inclusive). 

When we do this, we find a total of 11 lattice points inside the parabola:

$$(2, -\frac{3}{4})$$
$$(1, \frac{31}{4})$$
$$(0, 9)$$
$$(-1, \frac{31}{4})$$
$$(-2, -\frac{3}{4})$$
$$(3, -\frac{29}{4})$$
$$(2, \frac{1}{4})$$
$$(1, \frac{7}{4})$$
$$(0, 5)$$
$$(1, \frac{7}{4})$$
$$(2, \frac{1}{4})$$

Therefore, the total number of lattice points on or inside the region is $3+11=\boxed{14}$.

From $b+c=-3$, we get that $c=-3-b$ 

From $a+c= -5$, we substitute in our expression for $a$ and $c$ to get: 

$(8-b) + (-3-b) = -5$ 

Simplifying this equation, we get: 

$5 - 2b = -5$ 

Solving for $b$, we find that $b=5$

From $a+b=8$, we get that $a=3$ 

From $b+c=-3$, we get that $c=-8$

$abc = (3)(5)(-8) = -120$ 

Therefore, the value of the product $abc$ is $-120$.

Yes, I can certainly help you with your math problem! 

To solve this problem, we will use the formula for distance between two points in a Cartesian plane, which is $\sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2+(y_2-y_1)^2}$. 

First, we need to find the distance between the two given points. Let's call the first point $(x_1,y_1)$ and the second point $(x_2,y_2)$. 

$x_1 = 1, y_1 = -2, x_2 = -4$, and $y_2 = 10$ 
Distance between two points $= \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ 

Substituting the values into the formula, we get: 

$distance = \sqrt{(-4 - 1)^2 + (10 - (-2))^2} = \sqrt{(-5)^2 + (12)^2} \approx 13.0$. 

This distance represents the length of one side of the square, since the two given points are adjacent vertices. To find the perimeter of the square, we simply need to multiply this distance by 4. 

$perimeter = 4 \times distance = 4 \times 13.0 = 52$. 

Therefore, the perimeter of the square is 52 units.

Yes, of course! That problem is an example of a summation problem. To solve it, we can use the formula for the sum of an arithmetic sequence, which is:

$$S_n = \frac{n}{2}(a_1 + a_n)$$

where $S_n$ is the sum of the first $n$ terms of the sequence, $a_1$ is the first term, and $a_n$ is the $n$th term.

In this case, we can see that the sequence is an arithmetic sequence where the first term is 1, the common difference is 1, and the last term is 100. So, we can substitute these values into the formula to get:

$$S_{100} = \frac{100}{2}(1 + 100)$$

which simplifies to:

$$S_{100} = 50(101) = 5050$$

Therefore, the sum of the first 100 positive integers is 5050.

The points of intersection of the line $y = 7$ and $y = 2x^2 + 8x + 4$, by substitution, are found when $2x^2 + 8x + 4 = 7 \Longrightarrow 2x^2 + 8x - 3 = 0$. By the quadratic formula, $$x = \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{8^2 - 4 \cdot 2 \cdot (-3)}}{2 \cdot 2}.$$We want to find the difference of these roots to find the difference of the x-coordinates of the point of intersection, which will give a side length of the square. The difference is given by $\frac{\sqrt{8^2 - 4 \cdot 2 \cdot (-3)}}2 = \frac{\sqrt{88}}{2} = \sqrt{22}$. Thus, the area of the square is $\boxed{22}$.

Sure, I'd be happy to help you out with that math problem!

So, let's start by drawing a diagram to help us visualize the problem:

```
        |           .(B)
        |         .
        |       .
        |     .
        |   .
        | .(A)
        +------------------
                   |
                   |
                   |
                   |
                   |
                   |
                  (C)

```

Here, we have the parabola $y = 2x^2 + 8x + 4$ in blue, and the line $y = 7$ in red. The square we want to find has one side along the red line, and the other two vertices on the blue parabola. Let's label the three vertices of the square as $A$, $B$, and $C$, as shown in the diagram.

Now, let's find the $x$-coordinate of point $A$. Since the $y$-coordinate of $A$ is 7, we can set $y=7$ in the equation for the parabola and solve for $x$:

\begin{align*}
y &= 2x^2 + 8x + 4 \\
7 &= 2x^2 + 8x + 4 \\
2x^2 + 8x - 3 &= 0 \\
\end{align*}

We can solve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:

\begin{align*}
x &= \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\
&= \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{8^2 - 4(2)(-3)}}{2(2)} \\
&= \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{88}}{4} \\
&= \frac{-8 \pm 2\sqrt{22}}{4} \\
&= -2 \pm \frac{\sqrt{22}}{2} \\
\end{align*}

Since the square is symmetric about the $y=7$ line, we can take the positive value of $x$ to get the $x$-coordinate of $A$:

\begin{align*}
x_A &= -2 + \frac{\sqrt{22}}{2} \\
\end{align*}

Similarly, we can find the $x$-coordinate of $B$:

\begin{align*}
x_B &= -2 - \frac{\sqrt{22}}{2} \\
\end{align*}

Now, we can find the length of one side of the square by taking the difference in $x$-coordinates of $A$ and $B$:

\begin{align*}
AB &= | x_B - x_A | \\
&= |-2 - \frac{\sqrt{22}}{2} - (-2 + \frac{\sqrt{22}}{2})| \\
&= \sqrt{22}
\end{align*}

Finally, we can find the area of the square by squaring the length of one side:

\begin{align*}
\text{Area} &= AB^2 \\
&= (\sqrt{22})^2 \\
&= 22
\end{align*}

Therefore, the area of the square is $\boxed{22}$.

The height of the tree at time $t$ is given by the function $h(t) = 2t^2 - 5t + 29$.

To find the minimum height, we need to find the vertex of the graph of this function. One way to do this is to use the formula $t = -\frac{b}{2a}$, where $a$ is the coefficient of the quadratic term, and $b$ is the coefficient of the linear term.

In this case, $a=2$ and $b=-5$, so we have:

$$t = -\frac{-5}{2(2)} = \frac{5}{4}$$

$$h\left(\frac{5}{4}\right) = 2\left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^2 - 5\left(\frac{5}{4}\right) + 29 \approx 30\text{ feet}$$

So to the nearest foot, Ben's minimum height will be 30 feet off the ground.

Sure, let's solve this problem together!

To start off, let's define some variables:

- Let x be the number of rides Simon paid for. 
- Let y be the cost of the entry fee.

From the problem statement, we know that:

- Each ride costs $\$1.50$. Therefore, the total cost of the rides is $1.5x$.
- Simon spent a total of $\$12.75$. Therefore, we can set up the equation:

$$y + 1.5x = 12.75$$

We also know that the entry fee is $\$2.25$. Therefore, we can write:

$$y = 2.25$$

Substituting this into our first equation, we get:

$$2.25 + 1.5x = 12.75$$

Simplifying this equation, we get:

$$1.5x = 10.5$$

Dividing both sides by 1.5, we get:

$$x = 7$$

Therefore, Simon paid for 7 rides.

Sure, Let's solve the problem you provided! 

Given $\frac ab = \frac35$,$\frac bc=\frac{15}6$, and $\frac cd = 6$

We need to find the value of $\frac ad$ in simplest form.

To relate all fractions, we can use fraction multiplication.

We start with $\frac ab = \frac35$, then $\frac bc=\frac{15}6$, and finally $\frac cd = 6$.

\begin{align*}
\frac{a}{b} &= \frac{3}{5}      \\
\frac{b}{c} &= \frac{15}{6}     \\
\frac{c}{d} &= 6
\end{align*}

We can simplify $\frac{b}{c}$ by dividing the numerator and denominator by 3.

\begin{align*}
\frac{a}{b} &= \frac{3}{5}           \\
\frac{b}{c} &= \frac{5}{2}           \\
\frac{c}{d} &= 6
\end{align*}

Now, we can start multiplying fractions.

We'll start by multiplying $\frac{b}{c}$ and $\frac{c}{d}$.

$$\frac{b}{c} \cdot \frac{c}{d} = \frac{b}{d}$$

Substitute $\frac{b}{c}$ and $\frac{c}{d}$ with the equivalent fractions we found.

$$\frac{5}{2} \cdot 6 = \frac{15}{2}$$

Now, we can simplify $\frac{a}{b}$ by multiplying both the numerator and denominator by 2.

$$\frac{a}{b} = \frac{6}{10}$$

Substituting $\frac{a}{b}$ with the equivalent fraction we found, we have

$$\frac{6}{10} \cdot \frac{15}{2} = \frac{9}{2}$$

Certainly! 

First, we can simplify $\dfrac{\sqrt{10}}{\sqrt[4]{10}}$ as follows:

$$\dfrac{\sqrt{10}}{\sqrt[4]{10}} = \sqrt{10} \cdot \dfrac{1}{\sqrt[4]{10}}$$

Recall that $\sqrt{a} = a^{1/2}$ and $\sqrt[4]{a} = a^{1/4}$. Substituting this into the expression, we get:

$$\sqrt{10} \cdot \dfrac{1}{\sqrt[4]{10}} = 10^{1/2} \cdot 10^{-1/4} = 10^{(1/2 - 1/4)} = 10^{1/4}$$

Therefore, $\dfrac{\sqrt{10}}{\sqrt[4]{10}} = 10^{1/4}$.

By the Pythagorean theorem, the distance from $(2,n)$ to $(-1,1)$ is $\sqrt{(2-(-1))^2+(n-1)^2}$.  Setting this equal to $5$, we find \begin{align*}
9+(n-1)^2 &= 25 \implies \\
(n-1)^2 &= 16 \implies \\
n-1 = 4 \quad&\text{or}\quad n-1=-4 \implies \\
n = 5 \quad&\text{or}\quad n=-3.
\end{align*} Both of these solutions are integers, and their product is $\boxed{-15}$.

Sure, let's work on this problem together!

First, let's use the distance formula to write an equation based on the information given. The distance formula is:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

We know that the point $(2, n)$ is 5 units away from the point $(-1, 1)$. So we can substitute the coordinates of the two points into the formula:

$$5 = \sqrt{(2 - (-1))^2 + (n - 1)^2}$$

Simplifying, we get:

$$25 = (2 - (-1))^2 + (n - 1)^2$$
$$25 = 9 + (n - 1)^2$$
$$16 = (n - 1)^2$$

Taking the square root of both sides, we get:

$$n - 1 = \pm 4$$

Solving for $n$ in both cases, we get:

$$n = 5 \text{ or } n = -3$$

So the two possible integer values for $n$ are 5 and -3. And the product of these two values is:

$$5 \times (-3) = -15$$

Therefore, the product of all possible integer values for $n$ is -15.

Setting $x = 0,$ we get $f(0) = 9.$ Since $9 > 3,$ $f(9) = 9a + b.$ Hence, $$f(f(0)) = f(9) = 9a + b.$$But $f(f(x)) = x$ for all $x,$ so $9a + b = 0.$

Setting $x = 1,$ we get $f(1) = 7.$ Since $7 > 3,$ $f(7) = 7a + b.$ Hence, $$f(f(1)) = f(7) = 7a + b.$$But $f(f(x)) = x$ for all $x,$ so $7a + b = 1.$

Subtracting the equations $9a + b = 0$ and $7a + b = 1,$ we get $2a = -1,$ so $a = -1/2.$ From $9a + b = 0,$ we get $b = -9a = 9/2.$ Hence, $$a + b = -1/2 + (9/2) = \boxed{4}.$$

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's solve this problem!

First, we want to find $f(f(x))$ for all $x$ and set it equal to $x$:

\[f(f(x)) = f(\text{something}) = x.\]

To get to this point, we need to compute two compositions:

\[f(f(x)) = f(\,f(x)\,) = f(\text{something}).\]

We can split this into two cases depending on whether $x \le 3$ or $x > 3.$

Case 1: $x \le 3.$

In this case, $f(x) = 9 - 2x,$ so

\[f(f(x)) = f(\,9 - 2x\,) = \boxed{2x - 9}.\]

Case 2: $x > 3.$

In this case, $f(x) = ax+b.$ We can find $f(f(x))$ by substituting $ax+b$ into the expression for $f:$

\[f(f(x)) = f(\,ax + b\,) = a(ax + b) + b = a^2x + ab + b.\]

We want this to equal $x,$ so $a^2x + ab + b = x.$ Rearranging, we get

\[(a^2 - 1)x = (1 - a)b.\]

Since $x > 3,$ we can't have $a^2 - 1 = 0,$ so $a^2 \neq 1.$ Therefore, we can safely divide both sides by $a^2 - 1$ to get

\[x = \frac{1 - a}{a}b.\]

Now we substitute this into the expression $f(x) = ax+b$:

\[f(x) = a\cdot\frac{1 - a}{a}b + b = b - ab + b = 2b - ab.\]

Setting this equal to $x,$ we get

\[2b - ab = x = \frac{1 - a}{a}b,\]which simplifies to

\[2 - a = \frac{1 - a}{a}.\]

Multiplying both sides by $a$ and rearranging, we get

\[a^2 - 3a + 1 = 0.\]

Using the quadratic formula, we get

\[a = \frac{3 \pm \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]

(We reject the root $a = 1$ because then $f$ becomes the identity function, which doesn't have the property $f(f(x)) = x$ for all $x.$) Finally, substituting this value of $a$ back into the equation $2 - a = \frac{1 - a}{a},$ we can solve for $b:$

\[2 - \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} = \frac{1 - \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2}}{\frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2}},\]which simplifies to $b = \frac{1}{2}.$ Therefore, $a + b = \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} + \frac{1}{2} = \boxed{\frac{5 + \sqrt{5}}{2}}.$

We focus on the portion of the floor that is left unpainted. After one day, $\frac12$ of the floor is left unpainted. After two days, $\frac1{2^2}$ of the floor is left unpainted, and so on. After $n$ days, $\frac1{2^n}$ of the floor is unpainted. The floor has area $15^2 = 225$ square feet, so we look for the least number of days $n$ when at most $\frac1{225}$ of the floor is left unpainted: \begin{align*}
\frac1{2^n} &\leq \frac1{225}\\
\Rightarrow 2^n &\geq 225\\
\Rightarrow n&\geq8.
\end{align*} Thus, Zeno took $\boxed{8}$ days to paint the floor.

If the given quadratic equation has one solution, it follows that its discriminant must be equal to $0$. The discriminant of the given quadratic is given by $m^2 - 4(m+n)$, and setting this equal to zero, we obtain another quadratic equation $m^2 - 4m - 4n = 0$. Since the value of $m$ is unique, it follows that again, the discriminant of this quadratic must be equal to zero. The discriminant is now $4^2 - 4(-4n) = 16 + 16n = 0$, so it follows that $n = \boxed{-1}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's solve the problem! 

To have one real solution, the discriminant must be equal to 0: 

$$
b^2-4ac=0
$$

Substituting $a=1,$ $b=m,$ and $c=(m+n),$ we get:

$$
m^2-4(m+n)=0
$$

Simplifying this equation, we have:

$$
m^2-4m-4n=0
$$

We want this equation to have exactly one solution for $m$. This means that it must be a perfect square trinomial. We can complete the square by adding $4$ to both sides:

$$
m^2-4m+4=4n+4
$$

$$(m-2)^2=4(n+1)$$

Now we want to find a value of $n$ such that the expression on the right is a perfect square. Since $4(n+1)$ is always even, the value inside the parenthesis must be even too. For $(m-2)$ to be even, $m$ must be even. So let's write $m=2k$ for some integer $k$:

$$(2k-2)^2=4(n+1)$$

$$(k-1)^2=n+1$$

Therefore, $n+1$ must be a perfect square. This is true only when $n=3$. Otherwise we would have at least two different values of $m$ that satisfy the equation. 

So the answer is $\boxed{n=3}$.

As the center of the circle is at the point $(-5,2)$ and its radius is $r$, the equation for the circle is $(x+5)^2+(y-2)^2=r^2$. Expanding this, \begin{align*}
x^2+10x+25+y^2-4y+4 &= r^2 \\
x^2 + y^2+10x-4y &= r^2-29.
\end{align*}Now, this equation must match the form $Ax^2 + 2y^2 + Bx + Cy = 40,$ so we see that we can multiply the above by two so that the coefficients for $y^2$ match: $$2x^2 + 2y^2+20x-8y= 2r^2-58.$$Thus, $A=2$, $B=20$, and $C=-8$. Also, $2r^2-58=40 \Rightarrow 2r^2=98 \Rightarrow r^2=49$. As $r$ is the radius, it must be positive, so $r=7$.
Therefore, $A+B+C+r= 2+20-8+7= \boxed{21}$.

To solve this problem, we need to use the method of elimination, which involves adding or subtracting the equations to eliminate one of the variables. 

First, let's multiply the first equation by 729 and the second equation by -725:

$$(725\cdot 729)x + (725\cdot 727)y = 725\cdot 1500$$
$$(-725\cdot 729)x - (725\cdot 731)y = -725\cdot 1508$$

Simplifying each side gives:

$$529425x + 527475y = 1087500$$
$$-527475x - 530675y = -1093100$$

Now we can add these equations to eliminate $x$:

$$0x - 3300y = -5600$$

Solving for $y$ gives:

$$y = \frac{5600}{3300} = \frac{56}{33}$$

We can then substitute this value of $y$ into either of the original equations to solve for $x$. Let's use the first equation:

$$725x + 727\cdot \frac{56}{33} = 1500$$

Multiplying by 33 to eliminate the fraction gives:

$$23925x + 12312 = 49500$$

Subtracting 12312 from both sides gives:

$$23925x = 37188$$

Dividing by 23925 gives:

$$x = \frac{37188}{23925} = \frac{116}{75}$$

Finally, we can compute $x-y$:

$$x-y = \frac{116}{75} - \frac{56}{33} = \frac{112}{2475}$$

So the value of $x-y$ is $\frac{112}{2475}$, which is approximately 0.045.

If $101 \le x \le 110$, then notice that $10 = \sqrt{100} < \sqrt{x}< 11 = \sqrt{121}$. Thus, $\lfloor \sqrt{x} \rfloor^2 = 10^2 = 100$. The desired sum is then $(101 - 100) + (102 - 100) + \cdots + (110 - 100) = 1 + 2 + \cdots + 10 = \frac{10 \cdot 11}{2} = \boxed{55}$.

The absolute value of the product of two numbers is the product of their absolute values, so we can write  \[
|x^2-16|=|(x+4)(x-4)|=|x+4|\,|x-4|.
\]Since $|x^2-16|$ is written as the product of two positive integers, it is composite unless one of the integers is $1$.  Solving $|x+4|=1$, we observe that either $x+4=1$ or $x+4=-1$, which give solutions of $x=-3$ and $x=-5$.  Similarly, solving $|x-4|=1$ we find $x=3$ or $x=5$. Among the possible solutions $\{-5,-3,3,5\}$, only $\{-3,3\}$ yield a prime value for $|x+4|\,|x-4|$.  Therefore, the product of the integer values of $x$ for which $|x^2-16|$ is a prime number is $\boxed{-9}$.

Sure! Let's break this down into cases. 

First, if $|x^2-16|$ is equal to 2, then $x^2-16=2$ or $x^2-16=-2$. Solving for x, we get $x=\pm 2$. 

Second, if $|x^2-16|$ is equal to any other prime number (which must be odd), then either $x^2-16=p$ or $x^2-16=-p$, where $p$ is the prime number. Solving for $x^2$, we get $x^2=16+p$ or $x^2=16-p$. 

Now, we need to make sure that the values of $x$ obtained from each of these cases are integers. We can see that $x^2$ is always greater than or equal to 16, so $|x^2-16|$ is always greater than or equal to 0. This means that we can only have $|x^2-16|$ equal to a prime number if $x^2$ is greater than or equal to 16 and if $x^2$ is not equal to 16. 

For the first case, $x^2$ is equal to 16, so the only solution we get is $x=\pm 2$. 

For the second case, $x^2$ must be a perfect square greater than 16. The only such value that works is 25, which gives us $x=\pm 3$. 

Therefore, the two integer values of $x$ for which $|x^2-16|$ is a prime number are $x=2$, $x=-2$, $x=3$, and $x=-3$. The product of these values is $2\times -2 \times 3 \times -3 = \boxed{36}$.

3x + 2y &= 8\\
2y &= -3x + 8\\
y &= -\frac32 x + 4
\end{align*} Next, we can solve the second equation for $y$ as well: \begin{align*}
6x &= 2a - 7 - 4y\\
4y &= 2a - 7 - 6x\\
y &= \frac12 a - \frac74 - \frac32 x
\end{align*} 
Now we have expressions for $y$ in terms of $x$ for each equation. We know that the system has infinitely many solutions if and only if these two lines are coincident, or in other words, if they have the same slope and the same $y$-intercept. This means that $$-\frac32 = \frac12 a - \frac74.$$ 
We can solve this equation for $a$: \begin{align*}
-\frac32 &= \frac12 a - \frac74\\
-\frac12 &= \frac12 a\\
a &= \boxed{-1}.
\end{align*}
Thus, the constant $a=-1$ will make this system have infinitely many solutions.

There are two ways to do this quickly: Set $\frac{n^2-n}{2} = 55$, multiply both sides by 2, so that you have $n^2 - n = 110$.  Then, quickly notice that $n = 11$ is the only number conceivably close enough such that that equation will work (namely, $n = 10$ is too small, and $n = 12$ is too big, since 144 is way greater than 110.)  If you do the problem in this manner, you should do it all in your head so as to be able to do it more quickly (and you gain nothing from writing it out).

The other way is to quickly factor the numerator to $n(n-1)$, and once again multiply both sides by 2.  Then, you'll have $n(n-1) = 110$, from which you should recognize that both 10 and 11 are factors, from which you get $n = \boxed{11}$.

We can also solve this as a quadratic equation. $n(n-1) = 110$ becomes $n^2 - n - 110 = 0$. Factoring, we find that $(n - 11)(n + 10) = 0.$ This gives us $n = 11$ or $n = -10,$ but $n$ must be positive, so $n = \boxed{11}$.

Sure! Let $n$ be the number of teams in the round-robin tournament. The number of games played is $(n^2 - n)/2$, so $(n^2 - n)/2 = 55$. Multiplying both sides by 2, we get $n^2 - n = 110$. Yes, I can help you with that! Firstly, let's use the fact that $a,b,d$ form a geometric sequence to write $b$ in terms of $a$ and $d.$ Since $a$ and $d$ are consecutive terms of an arithmetic sequence, we know that $d-a = (d-b) + (b-a) = 2(b-a),$ and since $a,b,d$ form a geometric sequence, we know that $\dfrac{b}{a} = \dfrac{d}{b}.$ Putting this together gives you a system of equations:
\begin{align*}
b &= a\cdot r \\
d &= b\cdot r \\
d-a &= 2(b-a)
\end{align*}
where $r$ is the common ratio of the geometric sequence. Substituting $b=a\cdot r$ and $d=b\cdot r$ in the third equation gives $d-a = 2(a\cdot r - a),$ which simplifies to $d = 3a\cdot r - 2a.$ Now we want to find $a/d,$ which we can express in terms of $r.$ We have $a/d = \dfrac{a}{3a\cdot r - 2a} = \dfrac{1}{3r-2}.$ 
Therefore, to find $a/d,$ we just need to find $r.$ In a geometric sequence, the ratio between consecutive terms is always the same, so we know that $\dfrac{b}{a} = \dfrac{d}{b}$ implies $r^2 = \dfrac{d}{a}.$ But since $a,b,d$ form an arithmetic sequence, we know that $b-a = d-b,$ i.e. $d = a + 2(b-a) = 3a - b,$ so we can also express $r$ in terms of $a$ and $b.$ Substituting $b=a\cdot r$ and $d=3a-b$ gives $r = \dfrac{d-b}{a} = \dfrac{2a}{a} = 2.$
We've now found the common ratio $r=2,$ so we can plug this into our formula for $a/d$ to get $\dfrac{a}{d} = \dfrac{1}{3r-2} = \dfrac{1}{4}.$ Therefore, $\boxed{\dfrac{1}{4}}$ is the value of $\dfrac{a}{d}$ for the given conditions.

Let X be the number of correct answers and Y be the number of incorrect answers. As there are 100 questions in total and he answered 80 questions, we know that he did not answer 20 questions. Therefore:

$X + Y + 20=100$

$X + Y = 80$

As each correct answer scores 5 points and each incorrect answer scores -2 points, we can express his total score as:

$5X-2Y = 232$

We can use substitution method to solve the system of equations:

$X = 80 - Y$

$5(80 - Y) - 2Y = 232$

$400 - 5Y - 2Y = 232$

$3Y = 168$

$Y = 56$

$X = 80 - Y = 24$

Therefore, Frank answered 24 questions correctly.

Sure, let's begin. 

Since the quadratic has two complex roots of the form $\frac{-7\pm i\sqrt{15}}{8}$, we can express it in the form:
$$4x^2 + 7x + k = 4(x - \frac{-7+i\sqrt{15}}{8})(x - \frac{-7-i\sqrt{15}}{8})$$

Expanding this out, we can simplify to:
$$4(x - \frac{-7+i\sqrt{15}}{8})(x - \frac{-7-i\sqrt{15}}{8}) = 4(x^2 + \frac{7}{8}x + \frac{15}{16})$$
$$= 4x^2 + \frac{7}{2}x + \frac{15}{4}$$

Therefore, $k = \boxed{\frac{15}{4}}$.

\\ 2(x+5)(x+3)-3(x+3)(x+8)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (2x+10-(3x+24))(x+3)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (-x-14)(x+3)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (x+14)(x+3)&>0.
\end{align*} This inequality is satisfied if and only if $(x+14)$ and $(x+3)$ are either both positive or both negative. Sure! Let's start by simplifying the denominator first:

$$\begin{aligned} 3\sqrt{3}+7\sqrt{27} &= 3\sqrt{3} + 7\sqrt{3}\cdot\sqrt{9} \\ &= 3\sqrt{3}+7(3)\\ &=3\sqrt{3}+21. \end{aligned}$$

So the fraction can be rewritten as:

$$\begin{aligned} \frac{8}{3\sqrt{3}+ 7\sqrt{27}} &= \frac{8}{3\sqrt{3}+21}\\ &= \frac{8}{3(\sqrt{3}+7)}. \end{aligned}$$

Now, to rationalize the denominator, we can multiply both the numerator and denominator of the fraction by the conjugate of the denominator, which is $\sqrt{3}-7$:

$$\begin{aligned} \frac{8}{3(\sqrt{3}+7)} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}-7}{\sqrt{3}-7} &= \frac{8(\sqrt{3}-7)}{3(\sqrt{3}+7)(\sqrt{3}-7)}\\ &= \frac{8\sqrt{3}-56}{3(\sqrt{3})^2-7^2}\\ &= \frac{8\sqrt{3}-56}{3(3)-49}\\ &= \boxed{-\frac{8\sqrt{3}-56}{40}}. \end{aligned}$$

Therefore, the simplified and rationalized form of the fraction is $-\frac{8\sqrt{3}-56}{40}$.

Sure! Let's tackle this problem together. 

First, we need to notice that there are three variables, $a$, $b$, and $c$, and three equations given. This means that we have a system of linear equations, which we can solve using various methods such as substitution, elimination, or matrices. 

To save time, let's use elimination. First, let's rewrite the equations in standard form: 

\begin{align*}
ax + by + c &= x + 7, \\
a + bx + cy &= 2x + 6y, \\
cx + ay + b &= 4x + y.
\end{align*}

Now, let's eliminate $c$ by subtracting the first equation from the third equation multiplied by $c$: 

\begin{align*}
cx + ay + b - c(ax + by + c) &= 4x + y - c(x + 7) \\
cx - cax + ay - cby - c^2 + b &= 4x + y - cx - 7c \\
(1 - ca) x + (-b + a) y + (-c^2 + b) &= 4x + y - 7c. 
\end{align*}

Similarly, let's eliminate $c$ by subtracting the second equation from the third equation multiplied by $b$: 

\begin{align*}
cx + ay + b - b(a + bx + cy) &= 4x + y - b(2x + 6y) \\
-b^2 x + (a - 2bc) y + (b - 6c) &= 4x + y - 2bx - 6by \\
(-b^2 + 2b) x + (a - 6b + 1) y + (-6b - 4) &= 0.
\end{align*}

Finally, let's eliminate $b$ by subtracting the second equation from the first equation multiplied by $b$: 

\begin{align*}
ax + by + c - b(a + bx + cy) &= x + 7 - 2bx - 6by \\
(-ab + 1) x + (1 - b^2) y + (7 - bc) &= x - 2bx - 6by \\
(-a - 2b + 1) x + (1 - b^2 + 6b) y + (7 - bc) &= 0.
\end{align*}

Now we have three equations with only two variables, which we can solve easily. Let's solve for $b$ and $c$ in terms of $a$: 

\begin{align*}
b &= \frac{a^2 + 6a - 1}{a^2 - 6a + 1}, \\
c &= \frac{7 - ba}{a}.
\end{align*}

We can substitute these expressions into any of the three equations and solve for $a$. For simplicity, let's use the first equation: 

\begin{align*}
ax + by + c &= x + 7 \\
a \left( x + \frac{a^2 + 6a - 1}{a^2 - 6a + 1} \right) + y \left( \frac{a^2 + 6a - 1}{a^2 - 6a + 1} - a \right) &= x + 7 - \frac{7a}{a^2 - 6a + 1} \\
\frac{a (a^2 + 7a - 6)}{a^2 - 6a + 1} + \frac{5a^2 - 1}{a^2 - 6a + 1} &= \frac{a^3 - 6a^2 + 1 + 7a - 7a}{a^2 - 6a + 1} + 7 \\
\frac{a^3 + a^2 - a + 6}{a^2 - 6a + 1} &= 0 \\
(a - 2)(a^2 - 4a + 3) &= 0.
\end{align*}

Therefore, $a = 2$ or $a = 1$ or $a = 3$. Plug each value of $a$ back into the expressions for $b$ and $c$ to find all possible solutions. For example, if $a = 2$, then 

\begin{align*}
b &= \frac{2^2 + 6(2) - 1}{2^2 - 6(2) + 1} = -\frac{11}{5}, \\
c &= \frac{7 - 2 \cdot \frac{11}{5}}{2} = -\frac{3}{5}.
\end{align*}

Therefore, $a + b + c = 2 - \frac{11}{5} - \frac{3}{5} = \boxed{\frac{2}{5}}$.

Note that $f(2)=5$ implies $f^{-1}(5)=2$. Applying $f^{-1}(x+4)=2f^{-1}(x)+1$ repeatedly, we have: \begin{align*}
f^{-1}(5)&=2 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(9)&=2f^{-1}(5)+1=5 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(13)&=2f^{-1}(9)+1=11 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(17)&=2f^{-1}(13)+1=23.
\end{align*}So $f^{-1}(17)=\boxed{23}$.

Sure, let's first recall that $f^{-1}$ is the inverse function of $f$, such that $f(f^{-1}(x))=x$ for all $x$ in the domain of $f^{-1}$.

Now, to solve this problem, let's start by using the given equation $f^{-1}(x+4)=2f^{-1}(x)+1$ for $x=f(2)$. We get:

$$f^{-1}(f(2)+4)=2f^{-1}(f(2))+1$$

or, using the fact that $f(2)=5$,

$$f^{-1}(9)=2f^{-1}(5)+1$$

Next, let's use the fact that $f^{-1}$ is the inverse of $f$ to rewrite $f^{-1}(9)$ and $f^{-1}(5)$ in terms of $f$. We get:

$$f(f^{-1}(9))=9$$
$$f(f^{-1}(5))=5$$

Since we don't know $f$ explicitly, we can't solve for $f^{-1}(9)$ and $f^{-1}(5)$ separately. However, we can use a clever trick to eliminate $f^{-1}(5)$ from the equation above. We can add $4$ to both sides of the given equation $f^{-1}(x+4)=2f^{-1}(x)+1$ to get:

$$f^{-1}(x+4)+4=2f^{-1}(x)+5$$

Now, replacing $x$ with $f(2)$, we get:

$$f^{-1}(9)+4=2f^{-1}(5)+5$$

which simplifies to:

$$f^{-1}(9)=2f^{-1}(5)+1$$

We can substitute this expression for $f^{-1}(9)$ in the previous equation we found:

$$f^{-1}(9)=2f^{-1}(5)+1=2(f^{-1}(9)-4)+1$$

Simplifying, we get:

$$f^{-1}(9)=2f^{-1}(9)-7$$

Solving for $f^{-1}(9)$, we get:

$$f^{-1}(9)=7$$

Finally, we can use the fact that $f(f^{-1}(17))=17$ to find $f^{-1}(17)$ in terms of $f^{-1}(9)$:

$$f(f^{-1}(17))=17$$

$$f^{-1}(9)+8=f^{-1}(17)$$

Substituting $f^{-1}(9)=7$, we get:

$$f^{-1}(17)=7+8=15$$

Therefore, $f^{-1}(17)=15$.

We make the variable $x$ the number of adult patrons and $y$ the number of children patrons. Since the movie theater is full and seats $100$, $x+y=100$.

Adult tickets sell for $\$9.00$ each, so a total of $9x$ dollars were collected from adults. Children tickets sell for $\$5.00$ each, so a total of $5y$ dollars were collected from children. A total of $\$640$ were collected, so $9x+5y=640$.

We now have two equations, $x+y=100$ and $9x+5y=640$. We now solve for $y$.

We multiply the first equation by $9$ so that we can eliminate the $x$ term: $9x+9y=900$. Then, we subtract the second equation from it to get $9x+9y-(9x+5y)=900-640 \rightarrow 4y=260 \rightarrow y=65$.

Therefore, $\boxed{65 \text{ children tickets }}$ were sold.

Let's set up an equation using the information given in the problem.

Let x be the number of adult tickets sold and y be the number of children's tickets sold. We know that the theater is full, so we can write:

$x + y = 100$

We also know that the theater collected $\$640$ in ticket sales, so we can write:

$9x + 5y = 640$

We now have a system of two equations with two variables. We can solve for one variable in terms of the other (for example, solve for $x$ in the first equation) and substitute into the second equation. Alternatively, we can use elimination or substitution to solve for both variables.

Let's use elimination. Multiply the first equation by 5 to get:

$5x + 5y = 500$

Now subtract this equation from the second equation to eliminate $y$:

$9x + 5y = 640$
$-(5x + 5y = 500)$
$4x = 140$
$x = 35$

So 35 adult tickets were sold. We can substitute this value back into the first equation to find the number of children's tickets sold:

$x + y = 100$
$35 + y = 100$
$y = 65$

Therefore, 65 children's tickets were sold.

Sure, I'd be happy to help you! Let's break this problem down into smaller steps to make it easier to solve.

First, we need to determine what $g(x)$ is. We know that $g(x) = f(2x) - 3$, and we know that $f(x) = 2x + 1$. So, substituting in for $f(x)$ we get:

$g(x) = (2(2x) + 1) - 3 = 4x - 1$

Next, we need to find $g(g(2))$. So, substituting in $x=2$ into the equation we just found for $g(x)$, we get:

$g(g(2)) = g(4-1) = g(3)$

Finally, substituting $x=3$ into $g(x) = 4x - 1$ we get:

$g(3) = 4(3) - 1 = 11$

So, $g(g(2)) = g(3) = 11$. I hope that helps! Let me know if you have any questions or if you need further explanation.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure! Let's solve the problem.

Let's call the initial error in position $\Delta x$ and the initial error in momentum $\Delta p$. According to Heisenberg's Uncertainty Principle, we have:

$$\Delta x \cdot \Delta p \geq \frac{h}{4\pi}$$

Now let's halve the error in momentum. This means $\Delta p$ becomes $\frac{1}{2} \Delta p$. We want to find the new error in position, which we'll call $\Delta x'$.

Since the uncertainty principle still holds, we have:

$$\Delta x' \cdot \frac{1}{2} \Delta p \geq \frac{h}{4\pi}$$

Solving for $\Delta x'$, we get:

$$\Delta x' \geq \frac{2\pi}{h} \cdot \frac{h}{8\pi} \cdot \Delta p$$

$$\Delta x' \geq \frac{\Delta p}{4}$$

So the new error in position is at least one-fourth the original error in momentum.

To find the increase as a percentage, we can use the formula:

$$\text{percentage increase} = \frac{\text{increase}}{\text{original value}} \cdot 100\%$$

In this case, the increase is:

$$\Delta x' -\Delta x = \frac{\Delta p}{4} -\Delta x$$

Dividing by the original value $\Delta x$, we get:

$$\frac{\Delta x' -\Delta x}{\Delta x} = \frac{\frac{\Delta p}{4}-\Delta x}{\Delta x}$$

Simplifying and multiplying by 100%, we finally get:

$$\text{percentage increase} = \left(\frac{\Delta p}{4\Delta x}-1\right) \cdot 100\%$$

Plug in the value for the initial error in position and the error in momentum, and you'll have your answer!

- 2000 = C

Therefore, a person needs 2000 calories in a day to fulfill their daily caloric requirement.

We see that $x^2 + 4x + 4 = (x + 2)^2$. If $x$ must be positive, we can see that this expression can take on the value of any perfect square that is greater than or equal to $(1+2)^2=9$. Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! 

Let's first manipulate the given expression: \begin{align*}
x^2+4x+4 &= (x+2)^2 \\
&= (x+2)(x+2)
\end{align*}

Thus, we are interested in solving the inequality: $$10<(x+2)(x+2)<50$$

To solve this inequality, we can break it down into two separate inequalities: $$10 < (x+2)(x+2) \quad \text{and} \quad(x+2)(x+2)< 50,$$ then solve each inequality separately. Since the two inequalities have identical factors $(x + 2)^2$, we will get the same set of solutions for both.

1) For $10<(x+2)(x+2)$, we have $(x+2)(x+2)>0$ since the product of two positive numbers is again positive. Thus, we only need to consider: $$10<(x+2)(x+2)$$ which simplifies to $$0 < (x+2)(x+2) - 10 \quad \Rightarrow \quad 0 < x^2+4x-6.$$ We can solve this quadratic inequality by factoring the quadratic, noting that $x^2 + 4x - 6 = (x - 1)(x + 5)$: \begin{align*}
x^2 + 4x - 6 &> 0 \\
(x-1)(x+5) &> 0.
\end{align*} The inequality holds when $x<-5$ or $x>1$, as the quadratic is negative in the interval $-5<x<1$ and positive elsewhere. 

2) For $(x+2)(x+2)<50$, we can write the inequality as $$0 < 50 - (x+2)(x+2) \quad \Rightarrow \quad 0 < -x^2-4x+46.$$ We can again factor the quadratic: \begin{align*}
-x^2-4x+46 &> 0 \\
-(x+2)(x-6) &> 0.
\end{align*} The inequality holds when $x<-2$ or $x>6$, since the quadratic is negative in the interval $-2<x<6$ and positive elsewhere.

Therefore, the solution set for both inequalities is $( -\infty,-5) \cup (1, \infty)$.

We have to make sure that our solutions are valid in the original inequality. Thus, we can plug in a value from each interval, say $x = -6$ and $x = 2$: $$x = -6 \quad\Rightarrow\quad 10<(-4)(-4)<50 \quad\Rightarrow\quad \text{true}$$

$$x = 2 \quad\Rightarrow\quad 10<(4)(4)<50 \quad\Rightarrow\quad \text{false}$$

Hence, the only solution that works is $x \in \boxed{(-\infty,-5) \cup (1, \infty)}$. 

Therefore, there are an infinite number of positive integers $x$ that satisfy the given inequality.

If $x^2 + kx + 15= (x+a)(x+b)$, then \[x^2 + kx + 15 = x^2 + ax +bx +ab = x^2 +(a+b)x + ab.\]Therefore, we must have $ab = 15$, and for any such $a$ and $b$, we have $k = a+b$.   There are four pairs of integers that multiply to 15.  They are 1 and 15 (which give $k=16$), 3 and 5 (which give $k=8$), $-1$ and $-15$ (which gives $k=-16$), and -3 and -5 (which give $k=-8$).  The product of these four possible values of $k$ is \begin{align*}
(16)(8)(-16)(-8)& = (2^4)(2^3)(-2^4)(-2^3)\\
& = 2^{4+3+4+3} \\&= 2^{14}\\& = 2^{10}\cdot 2^4 = (1024)(16) = \boxed{16384}.
\end{align*}

Certainly, I can help you out! 

We know that if $x^2 + kx + 15$ factors as $(x+a)(x+b)$, then we have:

$(x+a)(x+b) = x^2 + (a+b)x + ab = x^2 + kx + 15$

Therefore, we can set up the system of equations:

$a+b = k$
$ab = 15$

We can solve for $a$ and $b$ in terms of $k$ by substituting $b = k-a$ from the first equation into the second equation:

$a(k-a) = 15$

This simplifies to $a^2 - ka + 15 = 0$, which we can solve using the quadratic formula:

$a = \frac{k \pm \sqrt{k^2 - 60}}{2}$

For $a$ and $b$ to be integers, $k^2 - 60$ must be a perfect square. The factors of 60 are 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 10, 12, 15, 20, 30, and 60. We can then check which of these numbers, when added to 60, yield a perfect square. We find that only $4$ and $36$ do:

$k^2 - 60 = 4^2$
$k^2 - 60 = 36^2$

Solving for $k$ in each case gives us:

$k = 7$ or $k = -11$

The product of these constants is then $7 \cdot -11 = \boxed{-77}$.

Certainly! We know that $4,a,b$ forms a geometric sequence, so $\frac{b}{a} = \frac{a}{4}$ or $b = \frac{a^2}{4}$. Also, we know that $b,c,5$ forms an arithmetic sequence, so $\frac{5-b}{c- b} = \frac{c - b}{b - a}$. Substituting for $b$ using the first equation, we get $\frac{5 - \frac{a^2}{4}}{c- \frac{a^2}{4}} = \frac{c- \frac{a^2}{4}}{ \frac{a^2}{4} - a}$.

Multiplying both sides by the denominators and simplifying, we get $16c - 4a^2 = 5c^2 - 5a^2 - ac^2 - 20c + 16a$. Rearranging, we have $5c^2 + (ac^2 - 16c) + (5a^2 - 16a) - 4a^2 = 0$.

We can use the quadratic formula to solve for $c$: $c = \frac{16 - a^2 \pm \sqrt{a^4 + 32a^2 - 256}}{10}$.

To find the greatest three-digit number $abc$, we want to maximize $abc$, which is $100a + 10b + c$. We already know that $b = \frac{a^2}{4}$. Plugging this into the formula for $c$, we have $c = \frac{16 - a^2 \pm \sqrt{a^4 + 32a^2 - 256}}{10}$. We want to find the value of $a$ that maximizes $100a + 10\left(\frac{a^2}{4}\right) + c$. 

If we choose the positive square root, then $c$ will be less than $16-a^2$. Conversely, if we choose the negative square root, then $c$ will be greater than $16-a^2$. Therefore, we want to choose the negative square root to maximize $100a + 10\left(\frac{a^2}{4}\right) + c$. Plugging in the negative square root, we have:

$c = \frac{16 - a^2 - \sqrt{a^4 + 32a^2 - 256}}{10}$

$abc = 100a + 10\left(\frac{a^2}{4}\right) + \frac{16 - a^2 - \sqrt{a^4 + 32a^2 - 256}}{10}$

We can simplify this equation by multiplying everything by 40:

$4000a + 1000a^2 + 4(16 - a^2 - \sqrt{a^4 + 32a^2 - 256})$

$= 1000a^2 - 4a^2 + 64 + 160 - 4a\sqrt{a^2 + 32}.$

$= 996a^2 - 4a\sqrt{a^2 + 32} + 224$.

To maximize this expression, we need to find the value of $a$ that maximizes $996a^2 - 4a\sqrt{a^2 + 32}$. Taking the derivative and setting it equal to zero, we get:

$1992a - \frac{4a^2}{\sqrt{a^2 + 32}} = 0$

Simplifying, we get:

$1992a^3 - 4a^2\sqrt{a^2 + 32} = 0$

$a^2(498a - \sqrt{a^2 + 32}) = 0$

$a = 0$, or

$498a = \sqrt{a^2 + 32}$

$497a^2 = 32$

$a = \frac{4\sqrt{31}}{31}$

Since $a$ must be an integer, we choose the next lowest possible value of $a$: $a = 4$. Plugging this into the equation above, we get:

$996(4^2) - 4(4)\sqrt{4^2 + 32} + 224$

$= 1256$

Therefore, the greatest three-digit number $abc$ is $\boxed{125}$.

Sure! Let's call the amount Susan ends up paying after the discount $S$, and the amount Pam ends up paying after the discount $P$. 

We know that Susan gets a $25\%$ discount on her tickets, so she only pays $75\%$ of the full price. Therefore:
\begin{align*}
S &= 4 \cdot 0.75 \cdot \$20 \\
&= \$60
\end{align*}

Similarly, Pam gets a $30\%$ discount on her tickets, so she only pays $70\%$ of the full price. Therefore:
\begin{align*}
P &= 5 \cdot 0.70 \cdot \$20 \\
&= \$70
\end{align*}

To find the difference, we can simply subtract the amount Susan paid from the amount Pam paid:
\begin{align*}
P - S &= \$70 - \$60 \\
&= \$10
\end{align*}

So Pam pays $\$10$ more than Susan.

Sure, I can help you solve that problem! 

First, we want to find the centers and radii of the circles. We can complete the square to write the equations of the circles in standard form:

$(x-12)^2 + (y-16)^2 = 64$ and $(x+12)^2 + (y+16)^2 = 64$

So the centers of the circles are $(12, 16)$ and $(-12, -16)$, and their radii are both $\sqrt{64} = 8$.

To find the shortest distance between the circles, we can draw a line that connects their centers. This line will be perpendicular to the common tangent line at the point where the circles touch.

The distance between the centers is $d = \sqrt{(12-(-12))^2 + (16-(-16))^2} = 40$, so the line segment connecting them has length 40.

The common tangent line is parallel to the line connecting the centers and is equidistant from them. We can find the distance between the centers and the line by subtracting the radii from the distance between the centers:

$d_1 = d - 2r = 40 - 2(8) = 24$.

So the shortest distance between the circles is 24.

First, we factor the denominator, to get \[\frac{x^2+5x+\alpha}{x^2 + 7x - 44} = \frac{x^2 + 5x + \alpha}{(x - 4)(x + 11)}.\]If this fraction can be expressed as a quotient of two linear functions, then the numerator must have either a factor of $x - 4$ or $x + 11$.

If the numerator has a factor of $x - 4$, then by the factor theorem, it must be 0 when $x = 4$.  Hence, $4^2 + 5 \cdot 4 + \alpha = 0$, which means $\alpha = -36$.

If the numerator has a factor of $x + 11$, then it must be 0 when $x = -11$.  Hence, $(-11)^2 + 5 \cdot (-11) + \alpha = 0$, which means $\alpha = -66$.

Therefore, the sum of all possible values of $\alpha$ is $-36 + (-66) = \boxed{-102}$.

For the numerator to be factored as two linear expressions, it is necessary and sufficient for the discriminant of the corresponding quadratic equation to be a perfect square. Sure, let's solve this math problem! We're given that the function $h(x)=ax+b$ satisfies $h(1)=5$ and $h(-1)=1$. Using these two pieces of information, we can set up the following two equations:

\begin{align*}
a(1)+b&=5\\
a(-1)+b&=1
\end{align*}

Simplifying the equations, we get:

\begin{align*}
a+b&=5\\
-b+a&=1
\end{align*}

Now we can solve for $a$ and $b$ by adding the equations:

\begin{align*}
2a&=6\\
a&=3
\end{align*}

Substituting $a=3$ into one of the equations, we can solve for $b$:

\begin{align*}
3+b&=5\\
b&=2
\end{align*}

So the equation for $h(x)$ is $h(x)=3x+2$. To find $h(6)$, we simply substitute $x=6$ into the equation:

\begin{align*}
h(6)&=3(6)+2\\
&=20
\end{align*}

Therefore, $h(6)=20$.

The equation $x^2+y^2 - 6=6x+2y$ can be rewritten as $x^2-6x+y^2-2y=6$. Completing the square, this can further be rewritten as $(x-3)^2-9+(y-1)^2-1=6$. Moving the constants to the right side of the equation, this is $(x-3)^2+(y-1)^2=16$, which is the equation of a circle with center $(3,1)$ and radius $\boxed{4}$.

Sure, happy to help! 

To find the radius of the cookie, we must first rearrange the equation to put it in standard form, $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$, where $(h,k)$ denotes the center of the circle and $r$ denotes the radius. 

Expanding the left-hand side of the given equation, we get:
$$x^2 + y^2 - 6x - 2y - 6 = (x-3)^2 + (y-1)^2 - 10$$

So, the given equation can be rewritten as:
$$(x-3)^2 + (y-1)^2 = r^2$$
where $r^2 = 16$ (by comparing coefficients).

Thus, we have a circle centered at $(3,1)$ with radius $r = \sqrt{16} = 4$.

Sure, we can solve that problem. We know that the arithmetic mean of $x$ and $y$ is 7, so we have $\frac{x + y}{2} = 7$. Rearranging this equation gives $x + y = 14$.

Next, we know that the geometric mean of $x$ and $y$ is $\sqrt{19}$, so we have $\sqrt{xy} = \sqrt{19}$. Squaring both sides of this equation gives $xy = 19$.

We are asked to find $x^2 + y^2$, which when rearranged, can be expressed as $(x + y)^2 - 2xy$. Using the equations we developed earlier for $x + y$ and $xy$, we have $(x + y)^2 - 2xy = 14^2 - 2\cdot 19 = \boxed{168}$.

Sure! Here's how to solve the problem:

First, we need to simplify the expression $x^2+4x+4$:

$x^2+4x+4 = (x+2)^2$

So, we want to find the number of positive integer values of $x$ such that:

$(x+2)^2 < 20$

Taking the square root of both sides, we get:

$x + 2 < \sqrt{20}$

$x + 2 < 2\sqrt{5}$

Subtracting 2 from both sides, we get:

$x < 2\sqrt{5} - 2$

Using a calculator, we can find that:

$2\sqrt{5} - 2 \approx 1.472$

Since we're looking for positive integer values of $x$, we need to find the largest integer less than or equal to $1.472$. That integer is 1.

Therefore, the only positive integer value of $x$ that satisfies the inequality is $x=1$.

So, there is only 1 positive integer value of $x$ that makes the sum $x^2+4x+4$ less than 20.

Sure, let's solve this problem together.

We are given that the area of the base of the cone is 30 square units and its height is 6.5 units. We want to find the volume of the cone.

We can use the formula for the volume of a cone:

$V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$

where $B$ is the area of the base and $h$ is the height.

Substituting the given values, we get:

$V = \frac{1}{3}(30)(6.5)$

$V = \frac{1}{3}(195)$

$V = 65$

Therefore, the number of cubic units in the volume of the cone is 65.

Let the energy of a photon equal $E$ and the wavelength equal $\lambda$.  Since the wavelength is inversely proportional to the energy, the product $E\lambda$ must be equal to some constant, say $k$.  Given photons of red light with a wavelength of $7\times10^{-7}$, we can write: \begin{align*}
E(7\times10^{-7})&=k\\
\Rightarrow\qquad 7\times10^{-7}&=\frac{k}{E}
\end{align*} Now, we are asked to find the wavelength of a photon with 2000 times the energy of red light.  Substitute $2000E$ for $E$ in the original expression: \begin{align*}
(2000E)\lambda&=k\\
\Rightarrow\qquad \lambda&=\frac{k}{2000E}\\
&=\frac{1}{2000}\cdot\frac{k}{E}\\
&=\frac{1}{2\times10^3}\cdot7\times10^{-7}\\
&={3.5\times10^{-10} \text{ meters}}
\end{align*} Therefore, we have $a+b = \boxed{-6.5}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, I can help you with that problem!

We know that the energy of a photon is inversely proportional to its wavelength. That means if a photon has $2\times$ the energy, it will have half the wavelength, etc. 

Let's call the wavelength of the red light photon $\lambda_\text{red}$. Then we want to find the wavelength of the photon with $2000\times$ the energy, which we'll call $\lambda_\text{new}$. 

Since the energy is inversely proportional to the wavelength, we can write:

$$ \frac{E_\text{new}}{E_\text{red}} = \frac{\lambda_\text{red}}{\lambda_\text{new}} $$

where $E_\text{new}$ and $E_\text{red}$ are the energies of the new and red light photons, respectively.

We know that $E_\text{new} = 2000\times E_\text{red}$. Substituting:

$$ \frac{2000 \times E_\text{red}}{E_\text{red}} = \frac{\lambda_\text{red}}{\lambda_\text{new}} $$

Simplifying:

$$ 2000 = \frac{\lambda_\text{red}}{\lambda_\text{new}} $$

Multiplying both sides by $\lambda_\text{new}$:

$$ 2000\lambda_\text{new} = \lambda_\text{red} $$

Now we can solve for $\lambda_\text{new}$:

$$ \lambda_\text{new} = \frac{\lambda_\text{red}}{2000} $$

Substituting the given value of $\lambda_\text{red} = 7\times 10^{-7}$ meters:

$$ \lambda_\text{new} = \frac{7\times 10^{-7}}{2000} = 3.5\times 10^{-10} \text{ meters} $$

So $a=3.5$ and $b=-10$. Therefore, $a+b = 3.5 - 10 = -6.5$, and written as a decimal, that is $\boxed{-6.5}$.

Certainly! Let's call the number of years it takes to recuperate your costs $x$. 

The total cost of installing and maintaining the green technology over $x$ years is: 

$10,\!000 + 650x$ 

The total amount of money you save on energy bills over $x$ years is: 

$1200x$ 

So, in order for the green technology to pay for itself (i.e. for the savings on energy bills to be greater than or equal to the cost of installation and maintenance), we need: 

$1200x \geq 10,\!000 + 650x$ 

Simplifying this inequality, we get: 

$550x \geq 10,\!000$ 

Dividing both sides by 550, we get: 

$x \geq \frac{10000}{550} = 18.\overline{18}$ 

Since we can't have a fractional number of years, the minimum number of years it will take to recuperate your costs is 19.

Thus, it would take 19 years to recoup your costs.

Sure, we can solve the problem together. 

Let's start with the given information: 

When $x = 2$, the expression $a + bx$ has a value of $15$: 

$a + b(2) = 15$ 

When $x = 5$, the expression $a + bx$ has a value of $3$: 

$a + b(5) = 3$ 

Now we have two equations with two variables ($a$ and $b$), so we can solve for them. 

First, let's isolate $a$ in the first equation: 

$a + b(2) = 15 \Rightarrow a = 15 - 2b$ 

We can substitute this expression for $a$ in the second equation: 

$15 - 2b + b(5) = 3$ 

Simplifying and solving for $b$: 

$15 - 2b + 5b = 3 \Rightarrow 3b = -12 \Rightarrow b = -4$ 

Now we can substitute this value of $b$ back into the first equation to solve for $a$: 

$a + (-4)(2) = 15 \Rightarrow a = 23$ 

So $a + b = 23 + (-4) = 19$. 

Therefore, the answer is $19$.

Extracting Answer from: 
The expansion of $(x+m)^2-63$ is $x^2+2mx+m^2-63$, which has a constant term of $m^2-63$. This constant term must be equal to the constant term of the original quadratic, so $m^2-63 = 1$, which yields the possibilities $m=8$ and $m=-8$.

If $m=8$, then $(x+m)^2-63 = x^2+16x+1$. If $m=-8$, then $(x+m)^2-63 = x^2-16x+1$. Of these two possibilities, only the first conforms to our information that $b$ was a positive number. So, the original quadratic was $x^2+16x+1$, giving $b=\boxed{16}$.

$(x+m)^2 = x^2+bx+64$

$x^2+bx+1 = x^2+2mx+(m^2-63)$

$2m = b$

$2mx = 2mx+(m^2-62)$

$0 = m^2-62$

$m = \pm \sqrt{62}$

So $m = \sqrt{62}$ and $b = 2m = 2\sqrt{62}$. 

Therefore, the value of $b$ is $2\sqrt{62}$.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/algebra/905.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let the constant term of Charles's quadratic be $c$, and the constant term of Eric's quadratic be $d$. Then Charles's discriminant is $(4)^2-4(1)(c)=16-4c$, and Eric's discriminant is $(4)^2-4(1)(d)=16-4d$. We're given that $$\frac{\text{Discriminant}_{\text{Eric}}}{\text{Discriminant}_{\text{Charles}}}=\frac{\text{Constant}_{\text{Charles}}}{\text{Constant}_{\text{Eric}}},$$or $\frac{16-4d}{16-4c}=\frac{c}{d}$. Cross multiplying gives  \begin{align*}
d(16-4d)&=c(16-4c)\quad\Rightarrow\\
16d-4d^2&=16c-4c^2\quad\Rightarrow\\
4c^2-4d^2&=16c-16d\quad\Rightarrow\\
4(c+d)(c-d)&=16(c-d).
\end{align*}Since $c\neq d$, we know that $c-d\neq 0$, so we can cancel this term to find  \begin{align*}
4(c+d)&=16\quad\Rightarrow\\
c+d&=4.
\end{align*}Thus the sum of Eric's and Charles's constant terms is $\boxed{4}$.

Hi there, it's great to meet you! It sounds like you have a rigorous course schedule, but it's great that you have a favorite subject in English. As for your math question, I can definitely help you out.

First, let's start with some background information. A quadratic polynomial is a polynomial of the form $ax^2 + bx + c$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants. The discriminant of a quadratic polynomial is defined as $b^2-4ac$. This value provides information about the quadratic's roots; specifically, if the discriminant is positive, then the quadratic has two distinct real roots; if the discriminant is zero, then the quadratic has one real root (a double root); if the discriminant is negative, then the quadratic has two complex (non-real) roots.

Now let's proceed to the problem. Let Eric's polynomial be $x^2+4x+e$ and Charles's polynomial be $x^2+4x+c$, where $e$ and $c$ are constants. Since both quadratics start with $x^2+4x$, we can conclude that $a=1$ and $b=4$ for both polynomials.

Next, we are told that the ratio of the discriminant of Eric's polynomial to the discriminant of Charles's polynomial is equal to the ratio of Charles's constant term to Eric's constant term. In other words, we have the equation:

$$\frac{(4)^2 - 4(1)(e)}{(4)^2 - 4(1)(c)} = \frac{c}{e}$$

Simplifying this equation gives:

$$\frac{16-4e}{16-4c} = \frac{c}{e}$$

Cross-multiplying gives:

$$16e - 4ce = 16c - 4ec$$

Expanding gives:

$$16e - 4ce - 16c + 4ec = 0$$

Simplifying this equation gives:

$$4(e-c)(e+c-4)=0$$

Thus, either $e=c$ or $e+c=4$.

If $e=c$, then we have the sum of the constants is $e+c=2e=2c$. 

If $e+c=4$, then we do not have enough information to determine the value of the constants. 

Therefore, assuming that $e$ and $c$ are not equal, the sum of the constant terms is $2e = 2c$. 

I hope this helps and let me know if you have any further questions. Good luck with your studies!

Sure, let's work through this problem together!

We want to find the minimum value of $y$, which is achieved when $x$ takes on a certain value. To find this value, we can use a technique called "completing the square."

First, let's rewrite the equation for $y$:

$$
y = x^2 - 7
$$

Now, let's add and subtract a constant to the right-hand side in order to allow us to complete the square. We can add and subtract $7$:

$$
y = x^2 - 7 + 7 - 7 = (x^2 - 7) + 7
$$

Notice that we added 7 and subtracted 7, which doesn't change the value of the equation, but allows us to write it in a more helpful way.

Now, let's focus on the expression inside the parentheses, $x^2 - 7$. We want to write this as the square of a binomial. Specifically, we want to write it as $(x - h)^2$ for some value of $h$. To do this, we need to find the value of $h$ that makes $(x - h)^2 = x^2 - 7$.

Expanding the right-hand side of $(x - h)^2$, we get:

$$
(x - h)^2 = x^2 - 2hx + h^2
$$

Comparing this to $x^2 - 7$, we see that we need:

\begin{align*}
-2hx &= 0 \\
h^2 &= 7
\end{align*}

The first equation tells us that $h$ must be equal to $0$ or $x$, since those are the only values that make the product $-2hx$ equal to zero. But we're looking for the value of $h$ that makes $(x - h)^2 = x^2 - 7$, so we can ignore the case where $h = 0$.

The second equation tells us that $h$ must be equal to either $\sqrt{7}$ or $-\sqrt{7}$. We could choose either value, but let's choose $h = \sqrt{7}$ for now. Then we have:

\begin{align*}
(x - \sqrt{7})^2 &= x^2 - 2\sqrt{7}x + 7 \\
(x - \sqrt{7})^2 &= (x^2 - 7) + 2\sqrt{7}x
\end{align*}

Notice that the first quantity on the right-hand side is the same as the expression we started with, and the second quantity is a positive constant times $x$. This tells us that $(x - \sqrt{7})^2$ is minimized when $x = -\frac{2\sqrt{7}}{2} = -\sqrt{7}$.

So, the minimum value of $y$ is achieved when $x = -\sqrt{7}$. Plugging this value into the original equation, we get:

$$
y = (-\sqrt{7})^2 - 7 = -7
$$

Therefore, the minimum value possible for $y$ is $-7$, achieved when $x = -\sqrt{7}$.

Of course! Given the graph of $f(x)$ as shown in the problem, we are looking for the maximum value of $f^{-1}(x)$, which is the inverse of $f(x)$. To find the inverse, we need to switch the $x$ and $y$ values and then solve for $y$: \begin{align*}
y &= f(x) \\
x &= f^{-1}(y)
\end{align*} 

We can use this relationship to find the inverse of $f(x)$. First, let's find the inverse on each interval of $f(x)$ (the intervals bounded by the dots). On the interval from $x = 1$ to $x = 3$, $f(x)$ is a decreasing linear function, so its inverse will be an increasing linear function. Thus, on this interval, we have \begin{align*}
y &= f(x) = 2 - \frac{2}{3}(x-1) \\
x &= f^{-1}(y) = 1 + \frac{3}{2}(2-y)
\end{align*} 

On the interval from $x = 3$ to $x = 5$, $f(x)$ is an increasing linear function. Thus, on this interval, we have \begin{align*}
y &= f(x) = 3 - (x-3) \\
x &= f^{-1}(y) = 3 - (y-2)
\end{align*}

On the interval from $x = 5$ to $x = 6$, $f(x)$ is a decreasing linear function. Thus, on this interval, we have \begin{align*}
y &= f(x) = 2 - \frac{2}{5}(x-5) \\
x &= f^{-1}(y) = 5 + \frac{5}{2}(2-y)
\end{align*}

Now we need to find the maximum value of $f^{-1}(x)$, which can come from any of these three intervals. Let's start with the first interval, $1 \le x \le 3$. The maximum value of $f^{-1}(x)$ on this interval occurs at $x = 1$, which corresponds to $y = 2$, so $f^{-1}(2) = 1 + \frac{3}{2}(2-2) = 1$.

Therefore, the maximum value of $f^{-1}(x)$ is $\boxed{15}$.
